===== PAGE 11 =====
Know Your Center
The Jacksonville Jewish Center is
“The Conservative Synagogue in Jacksonville”
THIRD AND SILVER STREETS PHONE EL 6-1504
For over half a century the Center has provided a modern
program in Jewish experience, ever increasing its services and
facilities. The current building program is described else¬
where in this book. Upon completion, with the help of God,
we shall have the most completely equipped Synagogue in
the area.
Over portions of several pages that follow, we review the
program of activities that is at the finger tips of all of our
loyal membership.
For the benefit of new members and newcomers to the
city, we set forth our program in brief. We also call atten¬
tion that the name "Center” is to be distinguished from
organizations by similar names in our communities that are
primarily recreational Centers. We are a "Synagogue-
Center.”
Religious Services
Daily: Mornings, 7:30 A.M. (8:30 on Sundays)
Evenings, at sunset (including the eves of Sabbaths
and Festivals)
Sabbath and Festivals:
Evenings, 8 o’clock
Mornings: Early service, 8:30; Reading of Torah, 9:30;
Musaf, 10 to 11
High Holidays: Members and their families, servicemen and
out-of-town guests are accommodated in our air-conditioned
Auditorium which seats 1400. (We do not "sell seats” as
the expression goes in some communities.)
Personal Observances
Our religious leadership supplements public worship with
services on personal occasions at the Synagogue and home,
such as: Circumcision, naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar
Mitzvah, Bas Mitzvah, Confirmations, Marriages, Conversion
to Judaism, Bereavement. The Cemetery Committee is in
charge of Memorial Parks located at Evergreen Avenue and
Winona Drive and at 40th Street, East of Main St.
Education
(Registration figures are as of the past year; registration
figures for the current year are running higher)
Daily Kindergarten: Monday to Friday, 9 to 12 noon. Four
teachers and assistants.
Week-day Hebrew School:
1. Primary Grades: "Play way to Hebrew,” for pupils 6
to 8 years of age, meets Monday and Wednesday, 2:45
to 4 o’clock. 37 pupils.
2. Elementary Grades: Boys and girls above age 8 are
taught on alternate days of the week, 3:30 to 5:30 or
4 to 6. 184 pupils.
3.Graduate class meets one afternoon and one evening
a week. 26 pupils.
Sunday Religious School:
Sunday mornings, 10 to 12; Pre-Kindergarten through 10th
Grade, Confirmation Class, 24 teachers, 591 pupils.
Junior Congregation:
Sabbath and Festival mornings at 10 o’clock.
Youth Services:
Sabbath and Festival mornings at 10 o’clock for pupils
above the 8th Grade. The worship is held in the Synagogue.
Minyan Club:
Sunday mornings at 8:45 for boys of Bar Mitzvah age.
Breakfast served at conclusion of the service by the Center
Men’s Club.
Adult Courses:
Institutes on specific subjects are arranged in convenient
units of study. In past years we offered "The Story of
Conservative Judaism” and the "Living Book Series.”
Special classes and discussion groups are arranged as called
for.
Affiliated Organizations and Activities
(see the section devoted to each in this Year Book)
Sisterhood, Men’s Club, Couples Club, Amity Club, Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Young People’s League—young adults, above
age 18, U.S.Y. (Center Youth League and Charles Palot
Club—teen-agers.)
Scouting: Boy Scouts and Cub Pack, Girls Scouts and
Brownies.
Athletics: Programs for youth and adults, men and women.
Social Events: Scheduled by the various organizations on
special occasions or in connection with regular worship
and meetings.
Community Service: The Center’s facilities are available not
only for the celebration of personal events, such as wed¬
dings, and Bar or Bas Mitzvahs, but are also used by youth
and adult organizations, civic groups and inter-faith groups.
Center Bulletin: The "Center Bulletin” appears weekly dur¬
ing the active season and is mailed to our membership
and to interested persons whose names and addresses are
submitted to the office.
Center Year Book: For a fuller description of the Center pro¬
gram and activities, the Center Year Book published an¬
nually on the eve of Rosh Hashanah offers a bird’s eye
view of events for the preceding 12 month period, vital
statistics of the membership, Hebrew Religious Calendar
for the coming year, directory of community organizations
and other departments.

Administration
MRS. BERNARD STEGMAN
MRS. NATHAN ROBIN
Office Address: 205 W. Third Street
Jacksonville 6, Florida
Telephone: ELgin 6-1504
MR. DAVID I. WEINSTEIN
As Administrator of an ever expanding Synagogue-Center which is serving more individuals and organizations than ever
before, Mr. Weinstein’s duties are manifold.
He executes policies adopted by the Officers and Board of Directors. He helps plan and implement the work and de¬
cision of many committees.
Specifically, he assists the Finance Committee in constant supervision of Center income and expenditure; the House Com¬
mittee by supervising the maintenance staff which includes Tim Riley, Morris Montford, Eugene Davis, Wallace Mazon,
Annie Mae Fort and Mary Williams, in the daily upkeep of the premises; the Membership Committee by preparing infor¬
mation for enrollment of new members.
His responsibilities include the scheduling of the many personal events in the lives of the membership and functions
of Center affiliated and non-affiliated organizations.
Together with the courteous and efficient office personnel, Mrs. Bernard Stegman, Mrs. Nathan Robin and Mrs. Esther
Fishier, our Administrator brings to the membership and the community as a whole the importance of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center.

Rabbis Message
The Cover Design
To the worshippers in our Synagogue the design of this
years cover for the Year Book must be familiar. It is the
Hanukah panel from the eight Festival carvings on the
Holy Ark of our Synagogue.
The elements in this carving were described briefly in
previous Year Books and in this edition among the notes
on the Calendar. The central motif is the well known Hanu¬
kah Menorah with its eight candles. Flanking the Menorah
and in shallower relief are a shield with the letter mem to
represent the Maccabee warriors and a cruse of oil that
figured in the miracle story how the sacred light was kept
going in the dededicated sanctuary. Hebrew lettering carved
at the top and at bottom spell out the initial words of the
Hanukah hymn, Ma’oz zur Yeshir’ati, "Rock of Ages, my
Salvation/'
Why Hanukah?
Cover design for the Year Book taken from the em¬
bellishments of our Holy Ark was initiated last year. Any
one of the eight carvings might have been appropriate for
the present edition. Why the Hanukah theme was selected
calls for explanation.
To be sure, Hanukah has become a very prominent ob¬
servance by American Jews. We need not stop to comment
on the factors and mode of life that gave this Festival unique
emphasis. More relevant is to recall the essential meaning
of the occasion. It is this meaning that prompted the use
of Hanukah symbolism on the cover of this Year Book.
Hanukah represents the victory of a small army fighting
for the religious freedom of a small people against the might
and tyranny of an empire. For the first time in history
freedom of worship won out and did so against overwhelm¬
ing odds. At the present stage in human history it is hardly
necessary to elaborate on the significance of freedom, religious
freedom in particular, in the growth of the spirit; that moral
strength stems from generous emotions towards fellow hu¬
man beings.
Religion in a Democracy
During the past year, the space of time chronicled by this
Year Book, our Congregation the Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter was the target of violence. Our building sustained slight
DR. SANDERS A. TOFIELD
damage in a series of similar unscrupulous efforts in this
area of the country to cause confusion and to cast onus on
a small religious community. Almost in answer to this luna¬
tic action was the groundbreaking for an addition to our
buildings that took place a week later but planned previously.
Hanukah has taught us over the centuries not to be
terrorized. Freedom of worship symbolized by the small
candles of the Menorah is a blessing it brought to the entire
world. Jewish citizens are not unaware or untouched by the
perplexing social and political problem of our region. The
words of the Prophet recalled in the lesson of Hanukah "Not
by power, nor by might, but by my spirit,” we advance as a
guide to our immediate concern.
In more contemporary language this position was ex¬
pressed by George Santayana, "Nothing will repay a man
for becoming human . . . neither prosperity nor empire nor
Heaven can be worth winning at the price of a virulent
temper, bloody hands, an anguished spirit, and a vain hatred
of the rest of the world.”
Fortunately this is a lesson appreciated by the vast
majority of democracy-loving Americans. Reaction to the
attack on our Synagogue is ample evidence. With all other
God-fearing, humanity-loving Americans we shall continue
to uphold the religious principle that human problems can
be solved only by application of spiritual insight and the
desire to live by the word and will of God.
We emblazon the Hanukah symbol on the cover of this
book to bespeak our historic faith and conviction in the
historic episode for which we were singled out this past
year. Hopeful, prayerful and confident are we that the com¬
ing year shall witness brighter existence for us and our fellow
citizens. May the light of the Menorah shine upon a most
Joyous, Happy, Prosperous and Blessed New Year.
► Rabbi David C. Kogen, Director of Program for the United Syna¬
gogue of America and at the time Acting Executive Secretary of the
Rabbinical Assembly of America, at the annual Convention of the
latter organization.
At the 1958 Annual Convention of the Rabbinical Assembly Dr.
Tofield was elected Corresponding Secretary of the national body. He
is also Chairman of the Southeast Region.

===== PAGE 17 =====
President's Message
It is now eight months Since I became your
President. These have been truly exciting months for
me. They began with the annual Membership Meet¬
ing of the Congregation and continued with our In¬
stallation Banquet, the launching of our drive for
funds to finally complete the building program, the
groundbreaking ceremonies for tKé new School
Building. There were my personal “Simhas” |§§the
Bas Mitzvah of my daughter Gail, and the Con¬
firmation of my daughter Ellen. There were the
celebrations with good friends of weddings, Bar
Mitzvahs and Bas Mitzvahs. All of this in addition
to the many religious observances which regularly
take place in our Synagogue.
It is most revealing to stop and think about how
such a tremendous part of our lives is connected
with the Jacksonville Jewish Center. I am certain all
of our members, if they would take the time to re¬
view their experiences in their own minds, would
find the same close connection with the Center.
Very few of us who are presently members of
the Center were here when the congregation was
organized-back at the turn of the century. However,
we can be sure that there was a group of men who
felt the need and the desire to found a synagogue
for their mutual benefit. They wanted a place to
worship, a place to give their children Hebrew
education and religious training, a place to observe
the many Simhas and solemn occasions in their lives
which are so closely tied to religious ritual. So they
organized a synagogue and each contributed what
they could to maintain it — some gave administra¬
tive ability, some gave financial assistance, all gave
time and effort, j
The Jacksonville Jewish Center today undoubt¬
edly has grown far beyond the iJream’ of the most
farsighted of the original founders. They could not
have envisioned the present size of our membership,
BURTON I. GROSSMAN
nor could they have dreamed of our beautiful Syna¬
gogue, our magnificent Activities Building or our
efficient School Building now under construction.
But, regardless of our size, we are still what the
founders intended us to be fpl a Synagogue for our
mutual benefit. We should keep in mind that this
is not a service provided for us by someone else, but
this is our Synagogue which we maintain for the
benefit of ourselves and our families. Mindful of
this fact, we can look forward to a New Year of ever
greater activity marked with that special enthusiasm
which each of us can feel in furthering a community
project in which we have a personal stake.
To all of our members I extend wishes for a
Happy and a Good New Year on behalf of all the
Officers and the members of the Board of Directors
and the Board of Trustees.
“Le Shanah Tovah Tikatevu”.

===== PAGE 19 =====
Chairman of the Board
This space in the Year Book is made available to me as Chair¬
man of the Board. I appreciate the honor and the opportunity. In
this particular capacity, however, I have little to add to what the
President of the Congregation placed before you in his well
chosen words on the previous page.
Let me have a few heart-to-heart words with you as Treasurer
of the Building Fund. At the present moment this function con¬
cerns me more than any other in the Center.
As you come to the building for services, take a look along the
Silver Street side. You will see how far we have progressed with
the construction of our new Classroom Building. If you look
around downstairs in the Synagogue Building, you will also ob¬
serve extensive remodeling.
This construction work was made necessary by the growth of
the Congregation and the increase of activities. Our children need
the extra space and accommodations.
Your share in this work goes without saying. We need your
contribution. Bills are mounting up. If you have not made your
pledge to the Building Fund, let the High Holiday season awaken
a sense of responsibility. The Torah Light Fund honoring names
of children and grandchildren is a worthwhile cause to support.
I want to wish each and every one of you a most happy New
Year. Help make the New Year happy for your children and
grandchildren.
ROBERT J. GORDON
Mr. and Mrs. Robert J. Gordon were presented with a silver tea service in appreciation of service to the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Mr*t
Joseph Hackel made the presentation at the Congregational Seder. Left to right Rabbi S. A. To field; Burton I. Grossman, President; Mrs.
Gordon, Mr. Gordon, Mr. Hackel and Cantor Abraham Marion.

Cantor and Choir
MESSAGE TO THE CENTER CHORAL SOCIETY
Dr. Sanders A. Tofield, Rabbi
Our age has been called the Age of the Spectator. Concentration into communities that
have followed in the wake of mechanized transportation has robbed the world of the inti¬
mate township with its opportunities for frequent meeting of friendly circles. In their place
has come the stupendous spectacle and more recently the remote performance.
Spectators are individuals who enjoy vicariously the skill and the talent developed by
others. Hundreds of thousands watch two teams in a stadium. The theatre and the concert
are smaller but they still convey to the many, the art expressed by a few. Listeners and view¬
ers of radio and television run into the millions.
The Center Choral Society shares in a world-wide movement to reverse the trend. Every¬
where small groups for self-expression are springing up. The Choral Society sponsored
by the Jacksonville Jewish Center strives to achieve even more.
On the 10th anniversary of its existence under the guidance and directorship of Cantor
Abraham Marton, the Congregation conveys its indebtedness to the Cantor and to all those
who have enjoyed musical expression from year to year and have helped others in the ap¬
preciation of the art.
Our Choral Society is motivated by distinct outlook. Furnishing an event of cultural significance to the Congregation and commun¬
ity is only part of the achievement. Educating the public to varieties and progress in the field of Jewish music are incidentals to the chief
function. We are a Jewish Congregation. Developing musical taste and stimulating inspiration from sacred tonal combinations in a wider
circle has been one of its accomplishments. Membership in the Choral Society is not confined to those who are blessed with exceptional
voices. The performance is not the ultimate objective. Cultivating appreciation for Jewish sacred and folk music among members of the
Congregation to whom this music is of greater significance has been and shall continue to be its chief goal.
Over the past decade I should estimate that over 100 individuals experienced the artistic satisfaction of blending their voices in pray¬
erful and meaningful harmony. With the intent of Hebrew salutation "Ye’yasher Ko’as”, may God lend effectiveness to your endeavors.
I greet Cantor Marton and the Choral Society as it enters its second decade in dedication to Jewish music.
Center Choral Society
Photographed at Center Choral Society Concert on Sunday, March 23rd in the Center Auditorium.
Center Glee Club
Pupils of the Center Religious School who sing in the Glee Club, at the Hanukah program.

Center Sisterhood
OFFICERS
President
Vice-Pres., Cultural
Vice-Pres., Youth
Vice-Pres., Fellowship
Vice-Pres., Ways & Means
Corres. Secretary
Recording Secretary

Treasurer
..Mrs. Sidney Stillman
Mrs. Charles Kluglose
... Mrs, Irving Kramer
Mrs. William Rose
. Mrs. Joe M. Witten
Mrs. Moe Zager
Mrs. Isidore Weiss
...Mrs. Henry Bettman
Mrs. Meyer Chepenik
...Mrs, Melvin Hollins
Mrs. Sidney Stillman
This year the Sisterhood instituted the wheel of Administration following National’s policy. Serving with the President are four
Vice-Presidents: Cultural, Youth, Ways and Means and Fellowship.
To date we have a membership of 539. Our projects this year included financial assistance for our Center Youth League and,
our Girl Scouts, provided funds for the salaries of the teachers in our Sunday Religious School, furnished prizes and awards for pupils
of the Hebrew and Sunday Schools and provided refreshments for special celebrations. In order to carry out our projects and programs
we have raised monies through dues, Bazaar, luncheons, sale of World Book Encyclopedias and the Sisterhood Book and Gift Shop.
This year’s "Donors for Beautification” was most successful and the funds raised will be used to purchase equipment for the new
school rooms now being erected.
Our Gift Shop had a very outstanding year and has been instrumental in fulfilling Sisterhood’s objective of strengthening Judaism
and making it more meaningful through the Synagogue and the Home.
Through the Torah Fund we contributed financial aid to the Jewish Theological Seminary.
This year we held Hanukah and Pesah Institutes which attracted a very large attendance. We are happy to report that as a result
of these Institutes more Hanukah Menorahs were lit this year and more Seders held in our homes.
Our Adult Education Committee cooperated with the Men’s Committee of our Center Synagogue in bringing four Rabbis to
Jacksonville for a series of lectures.
Sisterhood is affiliated with the National Women’s League from which we get guidance, information and suggestions enabling us
to fulfill our program. This year we sent delegates to the Regional Conference of the Southeast Branch of United Synagogues, held
in St. Petersburg, Fla.
In this year of the expansion of our Center, Sisterhood members are proud of the vital part that we share in its program and
growth.

The Men s Club
i
OFFICERS
President David R. Lewis
Vice-President Inman Leff
Secretary Edward Siegel
Treasurer Emanuel Ehrlich
Membership Co-chairman . . . Robert Wolf and Irwin Gendzier
DAVID R. LEWIS
The main objective of the Jacksonville Jewish Center Men’s Club is to stimulate social and cultural activities through the men
of the Center membership. These activities have been inter-related with certain religious and youth activities of the congregation.
Through the medium of monthly stag dinners we have been enjoying the benefit of timely informal talks from local political,
educational, sports and newsworthy figures.
The Men’s Club continues to support Cantor Marton with his guidance of the Minyan Club which is composed of Bar Mitzvah
age boys who meet on Sunday mornings for a special service and get-together breakfast. The Men’s Club sponsors the Bernard
Glossman Award for Boy Scout Troop 14, other awards to the Cub Scout Pack, and awards for Hebrew School and Sunday School
participation. Our annual Father-Son-Daughter Banquet is now one of our most popular events.
In conjunction with the Center’s continued building growth, the Men’s Club has pledged itself for even greater support for the
youth needs of our community, and for more social activities including the children.
We look forward to another year of advancement through the help and support of the men of our congregation who have shown
their great interest in the undertakings and activities of the Club.
Minyan Club Breakfast honoring Stephen and Stanley Chepenik on their Bar Mitzvah.
Awards presented to Boy Scouts at the Court of Honor held monthly in our Audi¬
torium,

The Couples Club
President Edward Siegel
Treasurer Edwin Presser
Secretary Mrs. Morris Mirkis
Trustees Mrs. Mike Donziger and Leo Jacobson
Edward Siegel
After six years as a successful social organization, the Couples Club has undergone a reorganization that has broadened
its scope and activities.
The group, open to all young married couples, now emphasizes not only social get-togethers and dances, but also in¬
formative programs and speakers, as well as entertaining special events.
Monthly affairs are planned for the members, and as part of its new format, special affairs and activities will also be
scheduled.
The club offers newcomers an opportunity of becoming a part of the community and provides all its members with last¬
ing friendships and varied experiences.

LOUIS A. TAXIER
The Mr. and Mrs. Club is a social club affiliated with the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Its membership is comprised of many old
Jacksonville residents and many newcomers to the city, and welcomes new members to join its ranks.
Interested parties are requested to contact any of the officers or members for details.
The Mr. and Mrs. Club has sponsored many enjoyable events since its inception five years ago. The Club has been instrumental in
forming numerous lasting friendships, and has provided sociability and entertainment for all who belong.
The outstanding events of the year were the Installation affair, the Invitational affair which is one affair where the membership
is permitted to invite local guests, and the New Year’s party. These affairs were very successful and were included in the regular fees.
Each month we have a small get-together and a large affair with a dance and dinner handled by capable committees from the member¬
ship.
Due to the cooperation on the part of the Officers and Board of Directors, as well as the various committees, the Club has been
established as one of the city’s leading social organizations.
Husbands and wives serve together on the various committees and Board to plan the social events.

United Synagogue Youth
Center Youth League (C Y L.)
C.Y.L. (Center Youth League) is an organization affiliated with United Synagogue
Youth, whose main interests are close association with the Synagogue plus a varied program
of cultural, service, social and athletic activities.
This year has been a very successful one for C.Y.L. We had 12 delegates participating
in the annual U.S.Y. Leadership Training Institute at Camp Blue Star, Hendersonville, N. C;
three delegates were sent to the National Convention held in Chicago, Ill.; 32 members went
to the Regional Convention in Charleston, S. C.
A few of our many programs were an interesting talk by our Cantor on his trip to Israel,
a discussion group on "Hanukah-Fact or Fiction,” Hanukah recreation night and latke supper,
an inter-faith dinner and three C.Y.L. Sabbath evening services with discussion groups fol¬
lowing.
This past April 18th our chapter was host for the Regional Board Meeting at which time
the new officers and committee chairmen planned the regional activities for the following
year.
Our year was closed with a big Installation Banquet. At this time the first annual awards
were presented for the outstanding boy and girl in C.Y.L. to George Glossman and Rene
Slott, respectively. The Banquet was followed by a semi-formal dance with an orchestra
and special entertainment.
Our deepest appreciation for their help during the past year goes to Mrs. Louis Slott
and Mrs. M. J. Reiser and our new advisors, Stanley Kantor and Joel Selber. We also wish
to thank the Center Sisterhood and the Charles Palot Club for their financial assistance in
sending delegates to L.T.I. and National Conventions.
George Glossman
Advisors of U.S.Y.: Stanley Kantor, Joel Selber
Mrs. Louis Slott and
Mrs. M. J. Reiser

The Charles Palot Chapter has accomplished many things this past year. A successful
Membership Drive Party started off the year. . . . On October 25 th we held our Friday night
Sabbath Service. Members of the Club helped Sisterhood at the Bazaar and participated in
the program and the selling of refreshments at the Hanukah celebration. . . . Last year’s
President, Judy Safer, represented the local chapter at the National Convention of United
Synagogue Youth in Chicago, Ill. Eight members attended ,the Southeastern Regional Con¬
vention in Charleston, S. C. . . . A Certificate of Merit was presented for our outstanding
work in the Muscular Dystrophy Drive. . . . The Charles Palot Club owes much of its
success to the able assistance of our Advisor, Mrs. M. J. Reiser.

Major's Pre-Teens and Tween-Agers
Two new clubs were organized this year for future members of the United
Synagogue Youth Clubs. . . . The Majors’ Pre-Teens was organized by Mrs. M. J.
Reiser and Mrs. Esther Fishier for 7th Graders who will be eligible next year
for the Charles Palot Club. Several social events were held. . . . The 5th and
6th Graders formed a club, the Tween-Agers, under the direction of Mrs.
Bernard Glossman, assisted by Mrs. I. Gartner. Social affairs included a Get-
together Party, Purim ball, Hobo party, picnic and installation of Officers.
Meetings are held every month under the sponsorship of the Charles Palot Club.

Boy Scout Troop 14
Scoutmaster Philip Selber
Assistant Scoutmasters Morris Leibovitz and Dr. Harry Yergin
Chairman Troop Committee Louis Slott
Jr. Assistant Scoutmaster George Glossman
Senior Patrol Leader Joe Borkson
Troop Committee: Rabbi S. A. Tofield, Milton Berman, Irving Franzblau, Joseph
Mizrahi, David Gradman, H. T. Freedman and the above.
The Center Boy Scout Troop 14 has had a very active program this past year.
The troop has gained quite a few members, but there is still room for more. Every
boy between the ages of 11 and 14 should avail himself of the program, as Scouting
offers so much to boys.
The year was highlighted by the attainment of the rank of Eagle by Junior
Assistant Scoutmaster, George Glossman, and the Bronze Palm by Byron Kolitz.
Simon Selber was also awarded the New Tamid, the highest religious award in
Scouting.
The year was climaxed by a week at Scout Camp, at which time Joe Borkson
and Nathan Franzblau were inducted into the Order of the Arrow, an honor
society for campers.
The troop is looking forward to bigger and better things in the coming year.
PHILIP SELBER
Scoutmaster

Cub Scouts Pack 14
The Cub Pack
C. C. GHELERTER
This was an outstanding year for the Cub Scouts of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, Pack 14. In February, 60 Cub Scouts were
registered at Scout Headquarters.
Pack meetings are held monthly at the Center. Each den prepared an exhibit for their monthly project on the theme of the month.
One of the successful events of the year was a Carnival held in the Park across the street from the Center. The entire Cub Pack
were guests of the Jacksonville Baseball Club. Another activity was the annual Father and Son Banquet.
Dr. Harry Yergin was Cubmaster the first half of the year. Clarence Ghelerter took over the Pack the first of February.
We look forward to another enjoyable year for the boys as well as parents.
Cub Scouts participating in Groundbreaking Ceremony.

===== PAGE 51 =====
Girl Scouts and Brownies
GIRL SCOUTS AND BROWNIES
Girl Scout Troop 212 and Brownie Troop 346 are sponsored by the
Center Sisterhood. Their leaders and co-leaders are happy to announce
that they had an interesting and profitable year.
The Girl Scouts met every Thursday after school on the grounds
of the J. C. A. in Arlington. They participated in the Scout Thinking
Day. It was an international program and they wore costumes of different
lands, presented a dance, and made an Israel flag. They participated in
the drive for funds for the Humane Society, painted tiles for Mother’s
Day presents, went horseback riding and took part in many outdoor
sports.
The Brownies were active this year also. They collected toys for
sick children at one of the hospitals, made special gifts for their parents,
held several cook-outs at their leader’s home, enjoyed a hay ride and
outing at a local dairy.
Both Brownie and Girl Scout troops had cook-outs at Boone Park.
They participated in the Ground Breaking Ceremony at the Center
for the new School Building. They took part in Friday Night Services
and later served as hostesses after the services. Arrangements were made
by Mrs. Irving Kramer, former Girl Scout Chairman, Mrs. Sidney Still¬
man who serves on the Girl Scout Board, and Mrs. Louis Slott, former
Girl Scout Leader.
MRS. IRVINE KOWITZ
COMMITTEE
Mrs. Mitchell Peltz, Mrs. Laurence Granet, Mrs. Stanley Zaret, and Mrs.
Saul Zavon.
Chairman Mrs. Irvine Kowitz
Leaders of Girl Scout Troop 212
Mrs. Lawrence Granet, Mrs. Stanley Zaret
Leaders of Brownie Troop 346 Mrs. Mitchell Peltz
Mrs. Saul Zavon
Closing Luncheon of Girl Scout Leaders at Biser’s Restaurant: Mrs. Irving Kramer; Mrs. Charles Kluglose, guest; Mrs. Saul Zavon;
Mrs. Sidney Stillman, Mrs. Mitchell Peltz, Mrs. Irvine Kowitz; Mrs. Lawrence Granet and Mrs. Stanley Zaret.
Girl Scout Family Group: Rabbi Tofield with Cynthia Stillman, Mrs. Sidney Stillman, Susan and Barbara Ann Stillman.

DAY CAMP
MRS. CORSON
Asst. Director
STAFF
This year’s staff also included Mrs. A1 Levy, in charge of Athletics;
Mrs. M. J. Reiser, Arts and Crafts; Miss Carol Vogel, Swimming; Assist¬
ant counsellors were Eddie Shmunes, Ada Hinda Block, Rene Slott and

MR. MAURICE CORSON
Director
The 1958 Summer Day Camp conducted by the Center included indoor and outdoor activities, swimming, field trips and daily
worship. Several times during the season parents were invited to p rogram events. The most prominent of them was the closing Lunch¬
eon on Friday, August 15 arranged by a committee of mothers.
Mr. Corson, Camp Director, is entering his third year in his studies for the rabbinate at the Jewish Theological Seminary of America.
Mrs. Corson is a teacher in the New York Public School system.

Adult Education
Rabbi Samuel Schaffler, Assistant Director of the
Commission on Jewish Education at the United Syna¬
gogue of America and Director of the Placement
Bureau, guest speaker at the annual Sisterhood Paid-
up Membership Tea, Wednesday, Feb, 12,
Dr, Albert I, Gordon, of Temple Emanuel, Newton
Center, Mass, spoke on the Cavalcade of Rabbis
series, Mon., March 17th on (fThe Jewish Community
of Tomorrow.”
Sefton David Temkin, Esq., of London, England,
gave a special lecture on February 10th on "World-
Wide Aspects of Conservative Judaism.”
Dr. Saul 1. Teplitz, Rabbi of the Laurelton Jewish
Center, gave talk on "What Should a Jew Do?”
Rabbi Stanley Rabinowitz of Adath Jeshurun Syna¬
gogue in Minneapolis, Minn, lectured on "A Phi¬
losophy of Judaism.”
Public Education
A number of church groups and public school classes have visited our Synagogue during the past year. Among these groups were
the Vacation Church School of Central Christian Church, Beach Methodist Church, North Shore Presbyterian Church, Murray Hill
Methodist and Arlington Methodist. The Arlington Kiwanis Club held a dinner meeting on June 9th. Several classes from Landon High
School, Hendricks Ave. and Terry Parker High School also visited our Synagogue.
A church group in our Synagogue. Rabbi Tofield is explaining re¬
ligious symbols to them.
A public school class in front of Synagogue building.

Community Organisations
On the pages that follow we list organizations active in the Jewish community of Jacksonville. The statements
of the respective organizations are edited only by the dictates of available space. In addition to those organizations
listed, there are two other Synagogues in the city: Congregation Ahavath Chesed, Reform; Congregation Etz
Hayim, Orthodox.
The Jacksonville Jewish
Community Council
HAROLD KATZ

HARRY GENDZIER
For the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council, 1958 has indeed been a year of decision. Faced with the bombing of the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center, the Council gave the community the type of leadership and solidarity needed in such a crisis. The manner in
which every Jewish organization in Jacksonville supported the Council has set a pattern for the nation and has resulted in widespread
respect everywhere for Jacksonville Jewry.
During this year our Executive Secretary, Ben Stark, resigned his position and we can now announce that the Executive Committee
has filled this post with Mr. Harold E. Katz, whose picture appears on this page with that of our President. Mr. Katz comes to Jackson¬
ville with an enviable record at Nashville and Los Angeles. Mr. and Mrs. Katz and their son and daughter will be with us sometime
during August.
Under President Gendzier’s dynamic leadership, Jacksonville Jewry will see a greatly widened horizon of community activities
which the Council will inaugurate. Studies are being continued on all phases of Council work and should result in a widened scope of
Community activity with more people participating in Council afiairs and a real upsurge in community and individual responsibility
in fund-raising for our local, national and overseas causes.

River Garden Hebrew Home for The Aged
Sidney Entman

Board Joe Becker, Robert N. Myers
Dr. Sam Witten
The twelve years of service that the River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged has given to the aged and infirm Jewish people of
Florida has given all of us a sense of security that those in need o f care can find a haven at the Home.
With the opening of the new addition last year the capacity of the Home has been increased to 75 residents. Five different wings
have been established in order to give individualized and personal care to the residents. Three of these wings provide nursing services
24 hours a day in order to meet their physical, mental and emotional disabilities. The other two wings have a homelike setting which
does not primarily feature the atmosphere of a nursing home. Recovery rooms have been provided in the nursing area to take care
of temporary illness for them.
In order to meet other temporary and varying needs of the aged the Home has inaugurated a program of Short Term Admissions.
This includes service to those who need limited convalescent care or who are recuperating from a serious illness or surgery; vacation
program for the aged whose families plan to be away from the city; and observance of the religious holidays for those whose religious
scruples prohibit traveling on the Holidays.
These added programs have broadened the scope of service of a modern Home for the Aged whose primary purpose is to serve
its community in as many ways as possible.

MRS. SAM SEIGEL
Officers
It is with a great deal of pride that we bring you greetings from the Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society, the auxiliary to
River Garden.
The Ladies Aid accepted the responsibility of providing the furnishings and equipment which have transformed River Garden
into a beautiful and comfortable home for the aged. But, just as important as our financial aid to the Home is the direct service we
try to give each and every one of the residents.
This year we have added many new projects to our program. The Blood Bank and Gray Ladies were both '’firsts” for the River
Garden. The Hobby Shop under the direction of a trained worker, the Ceramic project, the sewing group, the English and Hebrew
classes, the Home Newspaper, have all provided many happy hours to our many residents.
The financing of our many projects has been made possible through the wonderful cooperation of our fund raising committees:
the Social Printing project, Donors, Tombola, annual dues and a "Life Membership” in Ladies Aid.
The members of Ladies Aid hope that next year’s accomplishments will be even greater. We want to express our appreciation
for your cooperation in the past year.

Jewish Welfare Society

Lawrence Rackow
The program of the Jewish Welfare Society can be broken down into six overlapping categories: (1) Family consultation and
case work; (2) foster home and adoption placements; (3) Application processing for the River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged
and follow-up case work with the residents; (4) Resettlement, naturalization and migration assistance; (5) Transients; (6) Prison
program.
The family consultation and casework services continue to be the heart of the agency’s program as the great majority of cases
come for counselling, planning and other services that do not involve the direct outlay of funds. This past year there was a sharp in¬
crease in service to older people.
The agency placed three children for permanent adoption and one child in a foster home during 1957. The number of children
available for adoption puts the Jacksonville Jewish applicants in one of the most favorable positions in the United States.
The addition of a new worker, Mrs. Marcia G. Arnold, to the staff has made it possible for us to intensify casework with the resi¬
dents of the River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged and to broaden some of our other services.
There was considerable activity in the New American migration programs. The new Americans were among the first to feel the
effects of the recession and re-employment has been a difficult area to cover.
The number of transients helped during this past winter was markedly lower than other years, but with the return of normal weather,
this picture will change.
The agency, under the leadership of Rabbi Israel L. Kaplan, continued its work with the prisoners at Raiford.
The Jewish Welfare Society is the Family and Casework Agency of the Jewish community. It is a Community Chest — United
Fund agency with its local program mainly supported by the Chest. The Jewish Community Council provides two-thirds of the salary
of a social worker and assumes the entire cost of the resettlement of new Americans.
BEST WISHES FOR A HAPPY, HEALTHY & PROSPEROUS NEW YEAR

The National Council of Jewish Women is an organization "to bring about
a closer fellowship among Jewish Women, to further united efforts in behalf of
Judaism, to foster a program devoted to social welfare, community affairs, educa¬
tion and international understanding for peace.”
The Council supports the School of Education of Hebrew Univ. in Jerusalem,
sponsors scholarships for students from Israel for study in American universities,
aids Jewish orphanages in the Middle East.
In Jacksonville, the Council has such projects as the Medical Shelf, Sewing
Group, recreational program for the Childrens Home Society, Golden Age Club,
aid to troubled children, and gathering library books.
All Jewish women in Jacksonville are invited to join and participate in our
program. Meetings are held the third Monday of every month.
Mrs. Uly P. Mack

MRS. ALBERT STEIN
The National Womens Committee helps advance and promote the general welfare of Brandéis University in Waltham,
Massachusetts, as well as provide financial assistance.
In seven years 90 chapters have been established with a membership of 55,000. The money secured through member¬
ships and contributions to the Book Funds is used to meet all the needs of the University Library, so there is no fund- raising.
The Women’s Committee notes the development of Brandéis with pride. The women throughout the country have made
incalculable contributions by fostering good will for Brandéis in their respective communities. The university has expanded
its student body, gathered a distinguished faculty and realized its building program.

Zionist Organisation of America
Jacksonville District
President Cantor Abraham Marton
1st Vice-Pres Myer Cohen
2nd Vice-Pres Jack Dreher
3rd Vice-Pres Harry Libby
Treasurer Louis Moss
Secretary Charles Kluglose
Chairmen: Membership, David Moss;
Education and Program, Jack Becker;
Chairman of the Board, Max Rubin;
Israel Bonds, Max M. Zernes; Zionist
Public Relations, Leo Meizlik and L
Lieberman, Co-Chairman; Jewish Na¬
tional Fund, Jack Becker.
The Jacksonville Zionist District witnessed in the past year one of the most fruitful
years of public service and education to our community and the State of Israel. Several pub¬
lic meetings were dedicated to issues affecting Jewish life in America in relationship to the
people of Israel.
The annual Jewish National Fund Banquet was the most successful in recent years.
Guest speaker for the event was the former Mayor of Miami Beach, the Honorable Harold
Shapiro.
One of the best attended meetings of the year was the showing of the film "Hill 24
Doesn’t Answer” at the Prudential Auditorium. Cantor Marton was invited to appear before
several organizations to discuss Zionism, Jewish music and other Jewish topics of the day.
The American Jewish community is becoming more and more aware of the role that
organized Zionism has played in the last 50 years towards the creation of the State of Is¬
rael and are assuming more responsibility for the security and economic stability of the State.
Cantor Marton on behalf of Z.O.A. wishes to take this opportunity to extend to all a
Happy New Year. "Le Shanah Tovah Tikatevu.”
Cantor A. Marton

Young Judaea
Presidents of Young Judaea Groups
Estherettes
Rosalind Portnoy
Zionettes
Elaine Brukner
Judaeanettes
Mary Friedman
Israelites
Beverly Richman
Chaverim
Madelyn Levin
Israeli Dance Team at Bikkurim Festival
Jacksonville Young Judaea concluded a successful year under the direction of the Youth Commission with Mrs. Norman Moss
as Chairman and Mrs. Irving Brukner as City Director. There are three Junior Clubs, the Chaverim, Israelites and Judaeanettes, and
two Senior Clubs, the Zionettes and the Estherettes.
After a summer session at the Puldy home on Lake Brooklyn, the fall season opened with; excellent attendance. The St|te of Israel
was chosen as the theme for the year. The various clubs held an Oneg Shabbat, presented playlets, participated in fund-raising af¬
fairs and held a number of social events.
The Junior Clubs presented a Parents Day Program at the Center with a skit on Israel’s 10th Anniversary, Israeli dancing and
poems. Due to inclement weather the annual Israeli Fair at the River Garden Home was not held. The Young Judaea Sabbath took
place at the Jewish Center. Rosalind Portnoy gave the sermonette explaining Young Judaea’s activities in the city.
The 6th annual Bikkurim Festival and Banquet was held at the Center. Scholarships were given to Roxanne Mack and Byron
Kolitz /or Tel Yehudah and a scholarship to Linda Gruenberg for Machonah Avodah.
Israel dance groups were invited to perform at the Stephen Foster Memorial Festival in May.
We wish to take this opportunity to thank the following parents who served as leaders: Mrs. B. L. Friedman, Mrs. J. Parker,
Mrs. Abe Newman and Mrs. Charles Kluglose and the co-leaders, Miss Annette Gottlieb, Miss Karen Armel and Miss Linda Gruenberg.

Senior Hadassah
Jacksonville Chapter
President ..

Mrs. I. Moscovitz
The Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah is composed of approximately 800 Jewish women who meet monthly to serve together in
the ranks of the American movement. Meeting in the afternoon on the second Tuesday of each month, or in the evening with the Han¬
nah Senesch Group on the second Monday, Hadassah’s members join in efforts to affect the National Hadassah program of health and vo¬
cational education services to Israel; and as the second largest Womens group in the city, the local chapter participates actively in the
community life of Jacksonville.
With more than 315,000 members in 12 50 chapters and units in the United States, Alaska, and Puerto Rico, Hadassah is the world’s
largest Zionist organization. In Israel, Hadassah maintains seven hospitals, a school of nursing, the Hebrew University-Hadassah Medi¬
cal School, the New Hadassah-Hebrew University Medical Center, Youth Aliyah training farms, and the general youth medical services
emanating from the Jerusalem child health centers, together with a network of vocational education facilities which range from printing
to hotel management and fashion design. In cooperation with Z.O.A. Hadassah supports the Jewish National Fund for the rehabilitation
of the land of Israel and Zionist youth work through Young Judaea Clubs.
Highlights of the coming year will be the opening luncheon to be held September 9, the Membership Tea, an event of October 27
which will feature the new Israeli Fashion Show. The year’s activities will also include a "Dollars for Linen” shower for Hadassah hospital
supplies, a contributors party and the annual Donor luncheon in April.
HANNAH SENESCH GROUP
The Hannah Senesch Evening Group of Hadassah as part
of Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah has given those women
who can attend only evening meetings an opportunity to
participate fully in National Hadassah’s diversified program.
Membership is open to all Jewish women.

This year in conjunction with National B’nai B’rith, the A. B. Weil Lodge No. 800 will strive to increase its membership by 100
or more new members.
Our program for the year consists of several very vital community functions: the Little League Baseball Teams sponsored by
B’nai B’rith have been very active with members acting as coaches and managers and providing prizes for the winning teams; in con¬
junction with Brotherhood Week this fall, B’nai B’rith will sponsor an Essay contest in the High Schools; the Scholarship Loan Fund
is presently sponsoring several worthy recipients and we hope to be in a position to handle additional recipients. The B nai B rith
sponsors Anti-Defamation League, B’nai B’rith youth organizations such as A.Z.A., B.B. Girls, and Hillel.
Luncheon Meetings are held at the George Washington Hotel every two weeks. We have had very fine guest speakers. Come and
join us.

B’nai B’rith Women of Jacksonville have made great strides in membership due to the varied program which includes Anti-
Defamation, Philanthropy, Israel, Hillel or B’nai B’rith Youth.
Allocations are subscribed to nationwide projects which include the Leo N. Levi and the National Jewish Hospital, Bellefaire Chil¬
dren’s Home in Cleveland, the Children’s Home in Israel, the Great Martyrs Forest and two colonies in Israel,: Local projects include
work with the Pinecastle School for Retarded Children, Muscular Dystrophy Association, the Cancer Society, Tuberculosis and Cere¬
bral Palsy Associations and River Garden Home for the Aged.
Our youth organizations, the A.Z.A. and B’nai B’rith Girls’, are learning the B’nai B’rith principles for moral, ethical and health¬
ful living.

This past A.Z.A., the junior order of B’nai B nth, had a very successful year, one of the most outstanding in its history.
We published our first annual Yearbook which was distributed at our Sweetheart Dance. A great deal of credit for the success of our
social event is due our capable Sweetheart.
For community service projects we worked at the River Garden Home and are holding a raffle for the benefit of the Cancer Fund.
Our chapter had the largest delegation present at the North Florida Council Convention. Jerry Rothstein and Ronnie Levin were both
elected as officers. Jerry Rothstein was also elected reported at the Florida Regional Convention. Several members participated in the
District Convention at the University of North Carolina.
Gator Chapter 272 is looking forward to another successful year under its new administration and under the guidance of the
Advisor, Sam Silver.

The Jewish War Veterans of the United States is a national organization for Jews who have honorably served our coun¬
try in any of its wars. Organized in 1896, it continues to dedicate itself through comradeship and service to the highest
ideals of God and Country.
Post 199 participated in community affairs this past year, including the United Jewish Appeal and Israel Bond drives and
patriotic observances on Memorial Day and Veterans Day.
The Jewish War Veterans of the United States is "the patriotic voice of American Jewry.” A national Shrine to the Jew¬
ish War Dead is maintained at Washington, D. C.
The 63rd National Convention will be held at Los Angeles, Calif, during the week of August 3rd to August 10th, 1958.

Progressive Credit Union
OFFICERS Credit Committee: Louis Moss, Harry
Kline, Samuel Klepper; Supervisory
President Moe B. Safer Committee: Isadore Weiss, Herbert
Vice-President Israel Lipson Sandler, Samuel Ross; Directors: Max
I _ T TT Rubin, Louis Berinhout, Max Becker,
Secretary Eugene L Henry Sam Schuster, Sigmund Safier and
Treasurer Joseph Cohen Sam Gartner.
The Progressive Credit Union, sponsored by the Jewish Progressive Society, has con¬
tinued to grow in its 16 years of operation. It is one of more than 10,000 Credit Unions
in the United States, having more than 5 million members and more than one billion dol¬
lars in assets.
From a very modest beginning of 20 members and $500.00 in assets, we have grown
to over 350 members and more than $200,000.00 in assets. During our existence we have
made over 1600 loans to members, amounting to over $2,000,000.00 without a single loss.
We are organized to encourage thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium whereby
members can invest and accumulate savings. We are chartered by the State of Florida and
regularly examined by the Comptroller of the State, and the savings of members are safe¬
guarded in several ways.
Life Insurance equal to a memberV savings up to $1,000.00 and Loan Protection In¬
surance is paid by the Progressive Credit Union without any cost to the individual mem¬
ber. The climax of each year’s activities comes with the annual meeting held in the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center auditorium. Last year the Board declared a 2 % dividend on sav¬
ings and 15% rebate of interest to borrowing members.

THE JEWISH RELIGIOUS CALENDAR
GENERAL EXPLANATIONS
(See the notes for
each Hebrew month for explanations of Holidays, Festivals and other sacred occasions)
The Jewish Religious Calendar that appears on the fol¬
lowing pages of the Center Year Book is arranged according
to the months of the Civil Calendar, the Hebrew date ap¬
pears in the lower left corner under the large number.
This Calendar is the result of many stages of develop¬
ment. In ancient times the unit of time was the month, de¬
termined by the phases of the moon. The Hebrew Calendar
was thus lunar. However, since religious festivals are ob¬
served annually at given seasons the calendar also became
solar. The two systems of measuring time were harmonized
by means of leap years in the following manner. The lunar
month consists of 291/2 days. In seven of a 19 year cycle an
extra month is added.
The year 5719, for instance, has only 12 months while
the year before last year had a 13th month.
Framing the doors of the Holy Ark in our Synagogue as
illustrated on the previous page are a series of panels depict¬
ing eight sacred occasions of the Jewish year. These occa¬
sions are listed in the calendar and are described in the ex¬
planatory notes. Individual panel carvings appear with each
explanation.
The Sabbath is observed from Friday sundown to Satur¬
day sundown.
Sabbath. Two Candle¬
sticks and the Kiddush
cup; loaf of twisted Hal-
lah bread in the back¬
ground, inscription:
"Sabbath holiness”.
Fridays and the eve
of Festivals, as well as the first day of Festivals, carry the
symbol L. C, 'Tight Candles.” The hour that follows this
symbol is 18 minutes prior to sunset, based on information
furnished by the United States Weather Bureau for Jack¬
sonville.
The Sidrah, the portion of the Torah for the week, is in¬
dicated on Saturday's date. Special Sabbaths are named and
are explained below under the Hebrew month in which they
occur.
Rosh Hodesh, "Beginning of the Month”, is observed
with special prayers in the service for that day and with the
Blessing of the New Month on the Sabbath previous. When
the new moon appears in the middle of the day the outgoing
month consists of 30 days and the last day is considered the
first day Rosh Hodesh for the coming month.
The Meaning of Sacred Occasions
Month by Month
TISHRI (Sept.-Oct.)
Rosh Hashanah. Shofar,
with set of scales sym¬
bolic of divine judgment
in the background. In¬
scription: "Happy New
Year” on top; "Repent¬
ance” together with the
two Hebrew words in the next panel occur in the Unetane
To kef prayer.
Fast of Gedalia, commemorates the assassination of the
governor of Judaea who attempted to restore Jewish inde¬
pendence after the destruction of the First Temple by the
Babylonians,
Shabbat Shuva, Sabbath of Repentance, derives its name
from the Prophetic reading, "Shuva Yisrael,” "Return, O
Israel.”
Yom Kippur, Day of Atonement, is a day of fasting and
prayer, a day for confession of sin and supplication for for¬
giveness. "For transgressions between man and God, repent¬
ance on Yom Kippur brings atonement. For transgressions
between man and man, Yom Kippur brings no atonement
until amends are made.”
Yom Kippur. Prayer
book with the verse "On
this day shall Atonement
be made for you” inscrib¬
ed on its open pages;
Charity plate; with a tall
taper alongside. Inscrip¬
tion: "Prayer and Charity,” completing the quotation "Re¬
pentance, Prayer and Charity lead to Grace and Salvation”.
Sukkot, Tabernacles, commemorates the period when
our forefathers dwelt in booths (Sukkot) during their wan¬
dering through the wilderness of Sinai.
Sukkot. Lulav (palm
branch) set into a holder
with myrtle and willow
branches, an Etrog (ci¬
tron) lies alongside. In¬
scription: "Thou shall
rejoice in thy feast.”

===== PAGE 167 =====
HESHVAN (Oct.-Nov.)
No special religious occasions occur in this Hebrew
month.
KISLEV (Nov.-Dee.)
Hanukah, feast of Dedication, commemorates the reded¬
ication of the Temple by the Maccabees in 165 B.C.E. The
Maccabean triumph over Syrian forces was the first victory
for a peoples right to the freedom of worship. Lights are
kindled in the synagogue and in the home on every one of
the eight evenings.
Hanukah. Menorah with
candles; shield with let¬
ter "mem” for Maccabees
and cruse of oil in back¬
ground. Inscription:
"Rock of Ages, my Sal¬
vation.”
TEVET (Dec.-Jan.)
Asarah BeTebet, Fast of the 10th of Tebet, commemo¬
rates the beginning of the siege of Jerusalem by the Babylo¬
nians in 586 B.C.E. which led to the destruction of the first
Temple.
SHEBAT (Jan.-Feb.)
Hamisha Asar BeShebat, also known as Tu BeShebat,
15 th of Shebat, is Holy Land Arbor Day or New Year of
the Trees.
Shabbat Shekalim, the Sabbath when every adult Jew
in the days of the Temple was reminded to pay his poll tax
of a half a shekel on the first day of Adar.
ADAR (Feb.-Mar.)
Shabbat Zakhor. On this Sabbath before the Festival of
Purim a special portion of the Torah is read beginning with
the word "Zakhor”—"Remember Amaelk.” Haman is pre¬
sumed to have been an Amalekite.
Purim, commemorates the day when, through the inter¬
vention of Mordecai and Queen Esther, the Jews of Persia
narrowly escaped being victims of Hainan’s plot to extermi¬
nate them.
Purim. Megillah scroll of
Esther; in background,
crown and scepter. In¬
scription: "Peace to all
his seed.”
NISAN
Shabbat Hagadol, the Sabbath which precedes the great
Festival of Passover, derives its name from the Haftorah
which contains a reference to the ''Great Day of the Lord.”
Pesah, Passover, celebrates Israels deliverance from
Egyptian bondage. The festival lasts eight days during which
matzot are eaten in the place of leavened bread. The cele¬
bration of the Passover with the beautiful ceremony of the
(April-May)
Seder on the first and second evening has fostered by Jew¬
ish people the ideals of liberty and freedom.
Passover. Four cups, em¬
bellished with grape
leaves representing the
fourfold promise of re¬
demption. In back¬
ground, three matzohs.
Inscription: "Let my peo¬
ple go that they worship me.”
IYAR (May-June)
Yom Ha’atzma’ut. The new State of Israel declared its
independence on May 14, 1948, which was the 5th of Iyar
according to the Hebrew calendar.
Lag B’Omer, the date of a victory by Bar Kokhba in the
final Jewish struggle against the Romans. He was supported
by the sage Rabbi Akiba and his disciples. It is known as
"The Scholars Festival” and marked by field day outings.
SIVAN (June-July)
Shavuot, Feast of Weeks, completes 7 weeks from the
second day of Passover on which the Omer ( a measure ) of
the new barley was brought to the Temple. It was also the
day when Bikkurim (first fruits) were presented. Tradition
assigned to Shavuot commemoration of the Covenant estab¬
lished at Sinai between God and Israel and the giving of the
Ten Commandments.
Shavuot. Torah scroll in
decorated cover with sil¬
ver ornaments. In back¬
ground, stalks of wheat
and grapevines symbolic
of Bikkurim, first fruits.
Inscription: "Torah of
Truth.”
■¡1
TAMMUZ (July-August)
Shivah Asar BeTammuz, Fast of the 17th of Tammuz,
that commemorates the breach in the walls of Jerusalem by
the Babylonians in the Year 586 B.C.E.
AB (August-September)
Shabbat Hazon, precedes Tisha B’Ab and derives its
name from the first words of the Haftorah "Hazon”—the
vision of Isaiah, which foretells the destruction of Jerusalem.
Tisha B’Ab, the Ninth Day of Ab, is a fast day which
marks the destruction of the First Temple in Jerusalem by
the Babylonians in 585 B.C.E. and also of the Second Tem¬
ple by the Romans (70 C.E.)
Shabbat Nahamu, Sabbath of "Consolation”, the first
word of the Haftorah is "Nahamu,” Comfort ye, My People.
ELUL (September)
Selihot, "Penitential Prayers,” recited at midnight in
preparation for the holiday season, "The Great Days of
Awe.”

Know Your Center
The Jacksonville Jewish Center is
“The Conservative Synagogue in Jacksonville”
THIRD AND SILVER STREETS PHONE EL 6-1504
For over half a century the Center has provided a modern
program in Jewish experience, ever increasing its services and
facilities. The current building program is described else¬
where in this book. Upon completion, with the help of God,
we shall have the most completely equipped Synagogue in
the area.
Over portions of several pages that follow, we review the
program of activities that is at the finger tips of all of our
loyal membership.
For the benefit of new members and newcomers to the
city, we set forth our program in brief. We also call atten¬
tion that the name "Center” is to be distinguished from
organizations by similar names in our communities that are
primarily recreational Centers. We are a "Synagogue-
Center.”
Religious Services
Daily: Mornings, 7:30 A.M. (8:30 on Sundays)
Evenings, at sunset (including the eves of Sabbaths
and Festivals)
Sabbath and Festivals:
Evenings, 8 o’clock
Mornings: Early service, 8:30; Reading of Torah, 9:30;
Musaf, 10 to 11
High Holidays: Members and their families, servicemen and
out-of-town guests are accommodated in our air-conditioned
Auditorium which seats 1400. (We do not "sell seats” as
the expression goes in some communities.)
Personal Observances
Our religious leadership supplements public worship with
services on personal occasions at the Synagogue and home,
such as: Circumcision, naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar
Mitzvah, Bas Mitzvah, Confirmations, Marriages, Conversion
to Judaism, Bereavement. The Cemetery Committee is in
charge of Memorial Parks located at Evergreen Avenue and
Winona Drive and at 40th Street, East of Main St.
Education
(Registration figures are as of the past year; registration
figures for the current year are running higher)
Daily Kindergarten: Monday to Friday, 9 to 12 noon. Four
teachers and assistants.
Week-day Hebrew School:
1. Primary Grades: "Play way to Hebrew,” for pupils 6
to 8 years of age, meets Monday and Wednesday, 2:45
to 4 o’clock. 37 pupils.
2. Elementary Grades: Boys and girls above age 8 are
taught on alternate days of the week, 3:30 to 5:30 or
4 to 6. 184 pupils.
3.Graduate class meets one afternoon and one evening
a week. 26 pupils.
Sunday Religious School:
Sunday mornings, 10 to 12; Pre-Kindergarten through 10th
Grade, Confirmation Class, 24 teachers, 591 pupils.
Junior Congregation:
Sabbath and Festival mornings at 10 o’clock.
Youth Services:
Sabbath and Festival mornings at 10 o’clock for pupils
above the 8th Grade. The worship is held in the Synagogue.
Minyan Club:
Sunday mornings at 8:45 for boys of Bar Mitzvah age.
Breakfast served at conclusion of the service by the Center
Men’s Club.
Adult Courses:
Institutes on specific subjects are arranged in convenient
units of study. In past years we offered "The Story of
Conservative Judaism” and the "Living Book Series.”
Special classes and discussion groups are arranged as called
for.
Affiliated Organizations and Activities
(see the section devoted to each in this Year Book)
Sisterhood, Men’s Club, Couples Club, Amity Club, Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Young People’s League—young adults, above
age 18, U.S.Y. (Center Youth League and Charles Palot
Club—teen-agers.)
Scouting: Boy Scouts and Cub Pack, Girls Scouts and
Brownies.
Athletics: Programs for youth and adults, men and women.
Social Events: Scheduled by the various organizations on
special occasions or in connection with regular worship
and meetings.
Community Service: The Center’s facilities are available not
only for the celebration of personal events, such as wed¬
dings, and Bar or Bas Mitzvahs, but are also used by youth
and adult organizations, civic groups and inter-faith groups.
Center Bulletin: The "Center Bulletin” appears weekly dur¬
ing the active season and is mailed to our membership
and to interested persons whose names and addresses are
submitted to the office.
Center Year Book: For a fuller description of the Center pro¬
gram and activities, the Center Year Book published an¬
nually on the eve of Rosh Hashanah offers a bird’s eye
view of events for the preceding 12 month period, vital
statistics of the membership, Hebrew Religious Calendar
for the coming year, directory of community organizations
and other departments.

MR. DAVID I. WEINSTEIN
As Administrator of an ever expanding Synagogue-Center which is serving more individuals and organizations than ever
before, Mr. Weinstein’s duties are manifold.
He executes policies adopted by the Officers and Board of Directors. He helps plan and implement the work and de¬
cision of many committees.
Specifically, he assists the Finance Committee in constant supervision of Center income and expenditure; the House Com¬
mittee by supervising the maintenance staff which includes Tim Riley, Morris Montford, Eugene Davis, Wallace Mazon,
Annie Mae Fort and Mary Williams, in the daily upkeep of the premises; the Membership Committee by preparing infor¬
mation for enrollment of new members.
His responsibilities include the scheduling of the many personal events in the lives of the membership and functions
of Center affiliated and non-affiliated organizations.
Together with the courteous and efficient office personnel, Mrs. Bernard Stegman, Mrs. Nathan Robin and Mrs. Esther
Fishier, our Administrator brings to the membership and the community as a whole the importance of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center.

Rabbis Message
The Cover Design
To the worshippers in our Synagogue the design of this
years cover for the Year Book must be familiar. It is the
Hanukah panel from the eight Festival carvings on the
Holy Ark of our Synagogue.
The elements in this carving were described briefly in
previous Year Books and in this edition among the notes
on the Calendar. The central motif is the well known Hanu¬
kah Menorah with its eight candles. Flanking the Menorah
and in shallower relief are a shield with the letter mem to
represent the Maccabee warriors and a cruse of oil that
figured in the miracle story how the sacred light was kept
going in the dededicated sanctuary. Hebrew lettering carved
at the top and at bottom spell out the initial words of the
Hanukah hymn, Ma’oz zur Yeshir’ati, "Rock of Ages, my
Salvation/'
Why Hanukah?
Cover design for the Year Book taken from the em¬
bellishments of our Holy Ark was initiated last year. Any
one of the eight carvings might have been appropriate for
the present edition. Why the Hanukah theme was selected
calls for explanation.
To be sure, Hanukah has become a very prominent ob¬
servance by American Jews. We need not stop to comment
on the factors and mode of life that gave this Festival unique
emphasis. More relevant is to recall the essential meaning
of the occasion. It is this meaning that prompted the use
of Hanukah symbolism on the cover of this Year Book.
Hanukah represents the victory of a small army fighting
for the religious freedom of a small people against the might
and tyranny of an empire. For the first time in history
freedom of worship won out and did so against overwhelm¬
ing odds. At the present stage in human history it is hardly
necessary to elaborate on the significance of freedom, religious
freedom in particular, in the growth of the spirit; that moral
strength stems from generous emotions towards fellow hu¬
man beings.
Religion in a Democracy
During the past year, the space of time chronicled by this
Year Book, our Congregation the Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter was the target of violence. Our building sustained slight
DR. SANDERS A. TOFIELD
damage in a series of similar unscrupulous efforts in this
area of the country to cause confusion and to cast onus on
a small religious community. Almost in answer to this luna¬
tic action was the groundbreaking for an addition to our
buildings that took place a week later but planned previously.
Hanukah has taught us over the centuries not to be
terrorized. Freedom of worship symbolized by the small
candles of the Menorah is a blessing it brought to the entire
world. Jewish citizens are not unaware or untouched by the
perplexing social and political problem of our region. The
words of the Prophet recalled in the lesson of Hanukah "Not
by power, nor by might, but by my spirit,” we advance as a
guide to our immediate concern.
In more contemporary language this position was ex¬
pressed by George Santayana, "Nothing will repay a man
for becoming human . . . neither prosperity nor empire nor
Heaven can be worth winning at the price of a virulent
temper, bloody hands, an anguished spirit, and a vain hatred
of the rest of the world.”
Fortunately this is a lesson appreciated by the vast
majority of democracy-loving Americans. Reaction to the
attack on our Synagogue is ample evidence. With all other
God-fearing, humanity-loving Americans we shall continue
to uphold the religious principle that human problems can
be solved only by application of spiritual insight and the
desire to live by the word and will of God.
We emblazon the Hanukah symbol on the cover of this
book to bespeak our historic faith and conviction in the
historic episode for which we were singled out this past
year. Hopeful, prayerful and confident are we that the com¬
ing year shall witness brighter existence for us and our fellow
citizens. May the light of the Menorah shine upon a most
Joyous, Happy, Prosperous and Blessed New Year.
► Rabbi David C. Kogen, Director of Program for the United Syna¬
gogue of America and at the time Acting Executive Secretary of the
Rabbinical Assembly of America, at the annual Convention of the
latter organization.
At the 1958 Annual Convention of the Rabbinical Assembly Dr.
Tofield was elected Corresponding Secretary of the national body. He
is also Chairman of the Southeast Region.

President's Message
It is now eight months Since I became your
President. These have been truly exciting months for
me. They began with the annual Membership Meet¬
ing of the Congregation and continued with our In¬
stallation Banquet, the launching of our drive for
funds to finally complete the building program, the
groundbreaking ceremonies for tKé new School
Building. There were my personal “Simhas” |§§the
Bas Mitzvah of my daughter Gail, and the Con¬
firmation of my daughter Ellen. There were the
celebrations with good friends of weddings, Bar
Mitzvahs and Bas Mitzvahs. All of this in addition
to the many religious observances which regularly
take place in our Synagogue.
It is most revealing to stop and think about how
such a tremendous part of our lives is connected
with the Jacksonville Jewish Center. I am certain all
of our members, if they would take the time to re¬
view their experiences in their own minds, would
find the same close connection with the Center.
Very few of us who are presently members of
the Center were here when the congregation was
organized-back at the turn of the century. However,
we can be sure that there was a group of men who
felt the need and the desire to found a synagogue
for their mutual benefit. They wanted a place to
worship, a place to give their children Hebrew
education and religious training, a place to observe
the many Simhas and solemn occasions in their lives
which are so closely tied to religious ritual. So they
organized a synagogue and each contributed what
they could to maintain it — some gave administra¬
tive ability, some gave financial assistance, all gave
time and effort, j
The Jacksonville Jewish Center today undoubt¬
edly has grown far beyond the iJream’ of the most
farsighted of the original founders. They could not
have envisioned the present size of our membership,
BURTON I. GROSSMAN
nor could they have dreamed of our beautiful Syna¬
gogue, our magnificent Activities Building or our
efficient School Building now under construction.
But, regardless of our size, we are still what the
founders intended us to be fpl a Synagogue for our
mutual benefit. We should keep in mind that this
is not a service provided for us by someone else, but
this is our Synagogue which we maintain for the
benefit of ourselves and our families. Mindful of
this fact, we can look forward to a New Year of ever
greater activity marked with that special enthusiasm
which each of us can feel in furthering a community
project in which we have a personal stake.
To all of our members I extend wishes for a
Happy and a Good New Year on behalf of all the
Officers and the members of the Board of Directors
and the Board of Trustees.
“Le Shanah Tovah Tikatevu”.

Chairman of the Board
This space in the Year Book is made available to me as Chair¬
man of the Board. I appreciate the honor and the opportunity. In
this particular capacity, however, I have little to add to what the
President of the Congregation placed before you in his well
chosen words on the previous page.
Let me have a few heart-to-heart words with you as Treasurer
of the Building Fund. At the present moment this function con¬
cerns me more than any other in the Center.
As you come to the building for services, take a look along the
Silver Street side. You will see how far we have progressed with
the construction of our new Classroom Building. If you look
around downstairs in the Synagogue Building, you will also ob¬
serve extensive remodeling.
This construction work was made necessary by the growth of
the Congregation and the increase of activities. Our children need
the extra space and accommodations.
Your share in this work goes without saying. We need your
contribution. Bills are mounting up. If you have not made your
pledge to the Building Fund, let the High Holiday season awaken
a sense of responsibility. The Torah Light Fund honoring names
of children and grandchildren is a worthwhile cause to support.
I want to wish each and every one of you a most happy New
Year. Help make the New Year happy for your children and
grandchildren.
ROBERT J. GORDON

Cantor and Choir
MESSAGE TO THE CENTER CHORAL SOCIETY
Dr. Sanders A. Tofield, Rabbi
Our age has been called the Age of the Spectator. Concentration into communities that
have followed in the wake of mechanized transportation has robbed the world of the inti¬
mate township with its opportunities for frequent meeting of friendly circles. In their place
has come the stupendous spectacle and more recently the remote performance.
Spectators are individuals who enjoy vicariously the skill and the talent developed by
others. Hundreds of thousands watch two teams in a stadium. The theatre and the concert
are smaller but they still convey to the many, the art expressed by a few. Listeners and view¬
ers of radio and television run into the millions.
The Center Choral Society shares in a world-wide movement to reverse the trend. Every¬
where small groups for self-expression are springing up. The Choral Society sponsored
by the Jacksonville Jewish Center strives to achieve even more.
On the 10th anniversary of its existence under the guidance and directorship of Cantor
Abraham Marton, the Congregation conveys its indebtedness to the Cantor and to all those
who have enjoyed musical expression from year to year and have helped others in the ap¬
preciation of the art.
Our Choral Society is motivated by distinct outlook. Furnishing an event of cultural significance to the Congregation and commun¬
ity is only part of the achievement. Educating the public to varieties and progress in the field of Jewish music are incidentals to the chief
function. We are a Jewish Congregation. Developing musical taste and stimulating inspiration from sacred tonal combinations in a wider
circle has been one of its accomplishments. Membership in the Choral Society is not confined to those who are blessed with exceptional
voices. The performance is not the ultimate objective. Cultivating appreciation for Jewish sacred and folk music among members of the
Congregation to whom this music is of greater significance has been and shall continue to be its chief goal.
Over the past decade I should estimate that over 100 individuals experienced the artistic satisfaction of blending their voices in pray¬
erful and meaningful harmony. With the intent of Hebrew salutation "Ye’yasher Ko’as”, may God lend effectiveness to your endeavors.
I greet Cantor Marton and the Choral Society as it enters its second decade in dedication to Jewish music.
Center Choral Society
Photographed at Center Choral Society Concert on Sunday, March 23rd in the Center Auditorium.

This year the Sisterhood instituted the wheel of Administration following National’s policy. Serving with the President are four
Vice-Presidents: Cultural, Youth, Ways and Means and Fellowship.
To date we have a membership of 539. Our projects this year included financial assistance for our Center Youth League and,
our Girl Scouts, provided funds for the salaries of the teachers in our Sunday Religious School, furnished prizes and awards for pupils
of the Hebrew and Sunday Schools and provided refreshments for special celebrations. In order to carry out our projects and programs
we have raised monies through dues, Bazaar, luncheons, sale of World Book Encyclopedias and the Sisterhood Book and Gift Shop.
This year’s "Donors for Beautification” was most successful and the funds raised will be used to purchase equipment for the new
school rooms now being erected.
Our Gift Shop had a very outstanding year and has been instrumental in fulfilling Sisterhood’s objective of strengthening Judaism
and making it more meaningful through the Synagogue and the Home.
Through the Torah Fund we contributed financial aid to the Jewish Theological Seminary.
This year we held Hanukah and Pesah Institutes which attracted a very large attendance. We are happy to report that as a result
of these Institutes more Hanukah Menorahs were lit this year and more Seders held in our homes.
Our Adult Education Committee cooperated with the Men’s Committee of our Center Synagogue in bringing four Rabbis to
Jacksonville for a series of lectures.
Sisterhood is affiliated with the National Women’s League from which we get guidance, information and suggestions enabling us
to fulfill our program. This year we sent delegates to the Regional Conference of the Southeast Branch of United Synagogues, held
in St. Petersburg, Fla.
In this year of the expansion of our Center, Sisterhood members are proud of the vital part that we share in its program and
growth.

The Men s Club
i
OFFICERS
President David R. Lewis
Vice-President Inman Leff
Secretary Edward Siegel
Treasurer Emanuel Ehrlich
Membership Co-chairman . . . Robert Wolf and Irwin Gendzier
DAVID R. LEWIS
The main objective of the Jacksonville Jewish Center Men’s Club is to stimulate social and cultural activities through the men
of the Center membership. These activities have been inter-related with certain religious and youth activities of the congregation.
Through the medium of monthly stag dinners we have been enjoying the benefit of timely informal talks from local political,
educational, sports and newsworthy figures.
The Men’s Club continues to support Cantor Marton with his guidance of the Minyan Club which is composed of Bar Mitzvah
age boys who meet on Sunday mornings for a special service and get-together breakfast. The Men’s Club sponsors the Bernard
Glossman Award for Boy Scout Troop 14, other awards to the Cub Scout Pack, and awards for Hebrew School and Sunday School
participation. Our annual Father-Son-Daughter Banquet is now one of our most popular events.
In conjunction with the Center’s continued building growth, the Men’s Club has pledged itself for even greater support for the
youth needs of our community, and for more social activities including the children.
We look forward to another year of advancement through the help and support of the men of our congregation who have shown
their great interest in the undertakings and activities of the Club.
Minyan Club Breakfast honoring Stephen and Stanley Chepenik on their Bar Mitzvah.
Awards presented to Boy Scouts at the Court of Honor held monthly in our Audi¬
torium,

The Couples Club
President Edward Siegel
Treasurer Edwin Presser
Secretary Mrs. Morris Mirkis
Trustees Mrs. Mike Donziger and Leo Jacobson
Edward Siegel
After six years as a successful social organization, the Couples Club has undergone a reorganization that has broadened
its scope and activities.
The group, open to all young married couples, now emphasizes not only social get-togethers and dances, but also in¬
formative programs and speakers, as well as entertaining special events.
Monthly affairs are planned for the members, and as part of its new format, special affairs and activities will also be
scheduled.
The club offers newcomers an opportunity of becoming a part of the community and provides all its members with last¬
ing friendships and varied experiences.
